<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2019 51:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-05-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature America, Inc."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Like many other crops, the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) is of hybrid origin and has a polyploid genome that contains essentially complete sets of chromosomes from two ancestral species. Here we report the genome sequence of peanut and show that after its polyploid origin, the genome has evolved through mobile-element activity, deletions and by the flow of genetic information between corresponding ancestral chromosomes (that is, homeologous recombination). Uniformity of patterns of homeologous recombination at the ends of chromosomes favors a single origin for cultivated peanut and its wild counterpart A. monticola. However, through much of the genome, homeologous recombination has created diversity. Using new polyploid hybrids made from the ancestral species, we show how this can generate phenotypic changes such as spontaneous changes in the color of the flowers. We suggest that diversity generated by these genetic mechanisms helped to favor the domestication of the polyploid A. hypogaea over other diploid Arachis species cultivated by humans. The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut suggests that diversity generated by genetic deletions and homeologous recombination helped to favor the domestication of Arachis hypogaea over its diploid relatives."/>

itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jane-Grimwood" data-author-popup="auth-Jane-Grimwood">Jane Grimwood</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="HudsonAlpha Institute of Biotechnology" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0408 3720, grid.417691.c, HudsonAlpha Institute of Biotechnology, Huntsville, AL, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Peggy-Ozias_Akins" data-author-popup="auth-Peggy-Ozias_Akins">Peggy Ozias-Akins</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a>,<a href="#Aff13">13</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Georgia" /><meta Cannon</a></span><span class="u-js-hide"> 
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-2777-8034"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-2777-8034</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff23">23</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="US Department of Agriculture Agricultural Research Service" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0404 0958, grid.463419.d, Corn Insects and Crop Genetics Research Unit, US Department of Agriculture Agricultural Research Service, Ames, IA, USA" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na2">na2</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Scott_A_-Jackson" data-author-popup="auth-Scott_A_-Jackson" data-corresp-id="c2">Scott A.             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8062-9172"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-8062-9172</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a>,<a href="#Aff29">29</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="HudsonAlpha Institute of Biotechnology" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0408 3720, grid.417691.c, HudsonAlpha Institute of Biotechnology, Huntsville, AL, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Joint Genome Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0449 479X, grid.451309.a, Department of Energy, Joint Genome Institute, Walnut Creek, CA, USA" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na2">na2</a></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Like many other crops, the cultivated peanut (<i>Arachis hypogaea</i> L.) is of hybrid origin and has a polyploid genome that contains essentially complete sets of chromosomes from two ancestral species. Here we report the genome sequence of peanut and show that after its polyploid origin, the genome has evolved through mobile-element activity, deletions and by the flow of genetic information between corresponding ancestral chromosomes (that is, homeologous recombination). Uniformity of patterns of homeologous recombination at the ends of chromosomes favors a single origin for cultivated peanut and its wild counterpart <i>A. monticola</i>. However, through much of the genome, homeologous recombination has created diversity. Using new polyploid hybrids made from the ancestral species, we show how this can generate phenotypic changes such as spontaneous changes in the color of the flowers. We suggest that diversity generated by these genetic mechanisms helped to favor the domestication of the polyploid <i>A. hypogaea</i> over other diploid <i>Arachis</i> species cultivated by humans.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The domestication of plants, thousands of years ago, increased food supply and allowed the formation of large, complex human societies. Out of many thousands of wild species, only a few became domesticated crops and they now provide most of the food consumed by humans. It has long been noted that many of these crops are polyploid: their nuclei have more than two sets of chromosomes that are often derived from different species. Although it has been surprisingly difficult to rigorously demonstrate, it has long been thought that domestication may favor polyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Hilu, K. W. Polyploidy and the evolution of domesticated plants. Am. J. Bot. 80, 1494–1499 (1993)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1489">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Salman-Minkov, A., Sabath, N. &amp; Mayrose, I. Whole-genome duplication as a key factor in crop domestication. Nat. Plants 2, 16115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1492">2</a></sup>.</p><p>Peanut (also called groundnut; <i>Arachis hypogaea</i> L.) is an important food crop (annual production of ~44 million tons based on FAOSTAT data for 2016 (<a href="http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home">http://www.fao.org/faostat/en/#home</a>)). Whereas almost all related species in the genus <i>Arachis</i> are diploid (two sets of ten chromosomes; mostly 2<i>n</i> = 2× = 20 chromosomes), <i>A. hypogaea</i> is polyploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Husted, L. Cytological studies of the peanut Arachis. II. Chromosome number, morphology and behavior, and their application to the problem of the origin of the cultivated forms. Cytologia 7, 396–423 (1936)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1519">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fernández, A. &amp; Krapovickas, A. Cromosomas y evolución en Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 8, 187–220 (1994)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1522">4</a></sup>. The seeds of all of these species are an attractive food, and several have been cultivated for thousands of years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W. C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16, 1–205 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1526">5</a></sup> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a>). Indeed, the action of humans was key to the formation of <i>A. hypogaea</i> itself. About 9,400 years ago (estimated by nucleotide divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1536">6</a></sup>), the human transport of the ‘B’ genome species, <i>A. ipaensis</i> Krapov. &amp; W.C. Greg., into the range of the ‘A’ genome species <i>A. duranensis</i> Krapov. &amp; W.C. Greg. enabled their hybridization and the formation of <i>A. hypogaea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1549">6</a></sup>. It has two sets of chromosome pairs, one from each of the ancestral species: a type of polyploid termed allotetraploid (AABB-type genome; 2<i>n</i> = 4× = 40 chromosomes; genome size of ~2.7 Gb).</p><p>The origin of <i>A. hypogaea</i> was associated with a particularly severe population bottleneck<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kochert, G. et al. RFLP and cytogenetic evidence on the origin and evolution of allotetraploid domesticated peanut, Arachis hypogaea (Leguminosae). Am. J. Bot. 83, 1282–1291 (1996)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1562">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Seijo, G. et al. Genomic relationships between the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea, Leguminosae) and its close relatives revealed by double GISH. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1963–1971 (2007)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1562_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Moretzsohn, M. C. et al. A study of the relationships of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its most closely related wild species using intron sequences and microsatellite markers. Ann. Bot. 111, 113–126 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1565">9</a></sup>. This could, in principle, have reduced the variability on which, over generations, human selection could act. However, <i>A. hypogaea</i> evolved, becoming completely dependent on cultivation and morphologically very diverse<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W. C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16, 1–205 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1572">5</a></sup>. Two subspecies (<i>hypogaea</i> and <i>fastigiata</i>) and six botanical varieties (<i>hypogaea</i>, <i>hirsuta</i>, <i>fastigiata</i>, <i>vulgaris</i>, <i>aequatoriana</i> and <i>peruviana</i>) are recognized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W. C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16, 1–205 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1602">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. An overview of peanut and its wild relatives. Plant Genet. Resour. 9, 134–149 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1605">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Krapovickas, A., Vanni, R. O., Pietrarelli, J. R., Williams, D. E. &amp; Simpson, C. E. Las Razas de Maní de Bolivia. Bonplandia 18, 95–189 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1608">11</a></sup>. Different grain colors and sizes, pod shapes and growth habits distinguish thousands of landraces and cultivars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W. C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16, 1–205 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1612">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Krapovickas, A., Vanni, R. O., Pietrarelli, J. R., Williams, D. E. &amp; Simpson, C. E. Las Razas de Maní de Bolivia. Bonplandia 18, 95–189 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1615">11</a></sup> (see also United States Department of Agriculture (USDA) Germplasm Resources Information Network (<a href="https://www.ars-grin.gov">https://www.ars-grin.gov</a>)). It seems notable that, in spite of the higher genetic diversity of the diploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kochert, G. et al. RFLP and cytogenetic evidence on the origin and evolution of allotetraploid domesticated peanut, Arachis hypogaea (Leguminosae). Am. J. Bot. 83, 1282–1291 (1996)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1626">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Moretzsohn, M. C. et al. A study of the relationships of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its most closely related wild species using intron sequences and microsatellite markers. Ann. Bot. 111, 113–126 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1629">9</a></sup>, and their cultivation starting earlier (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a>), it was the derived allotetraploid, <i>A. hypogaea</i>, that underwent the transformation to become the crop of worldwide importance.</p><p>Some time ago, while planning to sequence and assemble the peanut genome, we realized that it would not be possible using the short-read data (~100–200 bp DNA) that were generated by the only technology that was economically feasible at the time; such sequences were too short to reliably resolve the very similar A and B genomes, which frequently have more than 98% DNA identity between corresponding genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1643">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ramos, M. L. et al. Chromosomal and phylogenetic context for conglutin genes in Arachis based on genomic sequence. Mol. Genet. Genomics 275, 578–592 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1646">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The repetitive component of the A genome of peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its role in remodelling intergenic sequence space since its evolutionary divergence from the B genome. Ann. Bot 112, 545–559 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1649">13</a></sup>. This level of similarity is due to the progenitor species that gave rise to the two subgenomes having diverged only around 2.2 million years ago (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1653">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Moretzsohn, M. C. et al. A study of the relationships of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its most closely related wild species using intron sequences and microsatellite markers. Ann. Bot. 111, 113–126 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1656">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nielen, S. et al. Matita, a new retroelement from peanut: characterization and evolutionary context in the light of the Arachis A–B genome divergence. Mol. Genet. Genomics 287, 21–38 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1659">14</a></sup>). Therefore, as a foundation for understanding the genome of cultivated peanut, we first sequenced the genomes of both the diploid ancestral species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1663">6</a></sup>. These diploid genomes afforded new insights into peanut genetics. Notably, it was possible to infer that some chromosome ends of <i>A. hypogaea</i> had changed from the expected AABB structure to AAAA or BBBB, implying a particular complexity in peanut genetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1670">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Leal-Bertioli, S. et al. Tetrasomic recombination is surprisingly frequent in allotetraploid Arachis. Genetics 199, 1093–1105 (2015)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1673">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Clevenger, J. et al. Genome-wide SNP genotyping resolves signatures of selection and tetrasomic recombination in peanut. Mol. Plant 10, 309–322 (2017)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1673_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nguepjop, J. R. et al. Evidence of genomic exchanges between homeologous chromosomes in a cross of peanut with newly synthetized allotetraploid hybrids. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 1635 (2016)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1673_2">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Leal-Bertioli, S. C. M. et al. Segmental allopolyploidy in action: increasing diversity through polyploid hybridization and homoeologous recombination. Am. J. Bot. 105, 1053–1066 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1676">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, using the much longer-read data obtained with PacBio technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Eid, J. et al. Real-time DNA sequencing from single polymerase molecules. Science 323, 133–138 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1684">19</a></sup>, and scaffolding using Hi-C<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1688">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1691">21</a></sup>, a method used for determining the conformation of DNA in the nucleus, we report the complete chromosome-scale genome sequence of <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner, a runner-type peanut. We also characterize the genomes of a diverse selection of cultivated peanuts, together with its wild counterpart, <i>A. monticola</i> Krapov. &amp; Rigoni, and induced allotetraploid hybrids derived from the ancestral species. We are able to visualize, in considerable detail, the products of variable deletions from, and genetic recombination between, the A and B subgenomes. It seems likely that these variations in genome structure generated phenotypic variation on which selection could act, and helped to favor <i>A. hypogaea</i> over its diploid relatives during the process of domestication.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly of the peanut genome</h3><p><i>Arachis hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Holbrook, C. C. &amp; Culbreath, A. K. Registration of ‘Tifrunner’ peanut. J. Plant Regist. 1, 124 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1718">22</a></sup>, a runner-type peanut (registration number CV-93, PI 644011) was sequenced using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Twenty chromosome sequences were produced (for assembly metrics see Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">2</a>). They were numbered Arahy.01–Arahy.20, where the A subgenome is represented as Arahy.01–Arahy.10 and the B subgenome as Arahy.11–Arahy.20. The chromosome sequences contain 99.3% of the assembled sequence and are 2.54 Gb, 93% of the size estimated by flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Samoluk, S. S., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, J. G. Genome sizes in diploid and allopolyploid Arachis L. species (section Arachis). Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 62, 747–763 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1728">23</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Chromosome architecture</h3><p>The chromosomes of <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner largely reflect their ancestral structures; the homeologous chromosomes mostly have a one-to-one correspondence: Arahy.02/12, 03/13, 04/14 and 10/20 are almost completely collinear; 06/16 and 09/19 are differentiated by a large inversion in one arm; 05/15 are differentiated by two large inversions; and 01/11 are differentiated by three large inversions. Chromosomes 17/18 have undergone reciprocal translocations relative to 07/08 (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">12</a>). Gene densities are highest in distal chromosome regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">13</a>). Gene counts are 11% higher in the B subgenome, with 35,110 predicted genes, compared to 31,359 genes in the A subgenome. Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons are highly abundant in pericentromeric regions, whereas DNA transposons are more frequent in euchromatic arms (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">14</a>). Other transposable elements, together with approximately 3,300 pararetrovirus sequences account for 74% of the assembled genome sequence (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">4</a>). Notably, this compares to 64% repetitive content estimated by reassociation kinetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Dhillon, S. S., Rake, A. V. &amp; Miksche, J. P. Reassociation kinetics and cytophotometric characterization of peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) DNA. Plant Physiol. 65, 1121–1127 (1980)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1762">24</a></sup>, indicating the high quality and relative lack of collapse of repeats in this long read-based assembly. The chloroplast genome of <i>A. hypogaea</i> and a chloroplastic plasmid were inherited from <i>A. duranensis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">DNA methylation and small RNAs</h3><p>Genic methylation patterns were typical for plants, with lower methylation in transcribed regions and characteristic dips in methylation at transcription start and end sites (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">16</a>). Genome-wide methylation per cytosine content was higher in pericentromeric regions than chromosome arms (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">17</a>). Methylation was lower in the A subgenome than the B subgenome; with 76.0% and 80.5% methylation at CG sites, 61.7% and 65.1% methylation at CHG sites (where H is an A, T or C) and 5.14% and 5.51% methylation at CHH sites, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">18a</a>). Greater densities of DNA sequences corresponding to small RNAs were found in proximal, repetitive-rich regions of chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">19</a>). However, greater densities of DNA sequences that corresponded to uniquely mapping small RNAs were found in gene-rich chromosomal regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">20</a>). Within genes, the B subgenome was enriched relative to the A subgenome for DNA sequences that corresponded to small RNAs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">18b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparison of gene expression in subgenomes</h3><p>The expression of homeologous gene pairs (dataset 1a in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1813">25</a></sup>) from the A and B subgenomes of Tifrunner was investigated in diverse tissues and developmental stages (dataset 1b,c in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1817">25</a></sup>). As has been reported in other recent polyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1821">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531–537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1824">27</a></sup>, overall, the number of homeologous gene pairs with expression biased towards the A subgenome was not significantly different from the number biased towards the B subgenome (<i>P</i> = 0.2, two-sided binomial test; <i>n</i> = 3,648 and 3,759 for A and B, respectively). However, when tissues were considered separately, all but one had slightly more B than A subgenome-biased genes from homeologous pairs. In three reproductive tissues and in roots this difference was significant (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, one-sided binomial test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">21</a>; dataset 1 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1841">25</a></sup>).</p><p>Broadly, homeologous pairs with the highest asymmetry in expression (log<sub>2</sub>(expression ratios) &gt; 3, Benjamini–Hochberg-adjusted <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, Wald test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">22</a>) were more commonly involved in oxidation–reduction processes, pollen recognition, lipid and chitin metabolic processes and response to biotic stimulus (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">23a</a>; dataset 1c in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1859">25</a></sup>).</p><p>Taking the example of the subterranean peg tip (a unique reproductive structure in peanut), the A subgenome-biased homeologous pairs were enriched for genes involved in mannose metabolic processes, nitrate assimilation and cell wall assembly, whereas the B subgenome-biased homeologous pairs were enriched for genes involved in the response to biotic stimulus, sucrose transport and glucan metabolic processes. In the maturing pericarp (Pattee stage 6), the A subgenome-biased homeologous pairs were enriched for genes involved in phosphorylation signal transduction, carbohydrate metabolism and cell wall biogenesis, whereas B subgenome-biased homeologous pairs were enriched for genes involved in inorganic ion transport and response to biotic stimulus (dataset 1d,e in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1866">25</a></sup>). Additionally, we identified homeologous gene pairs with the highest asymmetry in expression (<i>n</i> = 4,062; log<sub>2</sub>(expression ratios) &gt; 3, Benjamini–Hochberg-adjusted <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, Wald test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">22</a>) and a set of 394 pairs that displayed consistent asymmetrical expression patterns in at least half of the evaluated tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">23b</a>). Highly asymmetrically expressed homeologous pairs were more commonly involved in oxidation–reduction processes, pollen recognition, lipid and chitin metabolic processes and response to biotic stimulus (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">23a</a>, dataset 1c in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1888">25</a></sup>) and, as might be expected, the consistently asymmetrically expressed homeologous pairs were mainly enriched for functions associated with fundamental biological processes such as organelle organization, molecular transport and protein complex biogenesis (dataset 1c in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1892">25</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Changes following polyploidy</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec8">Genetic exchange between subgenomes and deletions</h4><p>For allotetraploids, chromosome associations during meiosis and genetic exchange are mostly limited to corresponding chromosomes within the same subgenome (that is, homologous chromosomes); however, as has been characterized in other plants such as <i>Brassica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1911">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, C. J. Homoeologous recombination in allopolyploids: the polyploid ratchet. New Phytol. 186, 18–28 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1914">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hurgobin, B. et al. Homoeologous exchange is a major cause of gene presence/absence variation in the amphidiploid Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1265–1274 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1917">29</a></sup>, these may also occur at lower frequency between corresponding chromosomes from the other subgenome (that is, homeologous chromosomes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Husted, L. Cytological studies of the peanut Arachis. II. Chromosome number, morphology and behavior, and their application to the problem of the origin of the cultivated forms. Cytologia 7, 396–423 (1936)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1921">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1924">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Clevenger, J. et al. Genome-wide SNP genotyping resolves signatures of selection and tetrasomic recombination in peanut. Mol. Plant 10, 309–322 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1927">16</a></sup>. We investigated genetic exchange between the subgenomes and deletions in more than 200 diverse genotypes comprising the wild tetraploid peanut (<i>A. monticola</i>), landraces and cultivars of <i>A. hypogaea</i>, and new allotetraploid hybrids made from the ancestral species (dataset 2 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1937">25</a></sup>). Two different approaches were used: observation of mapping densities of short-read whole-genome sequences onto the combined sequenced diploid ancestral species genomes, and analysis of the short-read whole-genome sequences for single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) that consistently differentiate representatives of A and B genome diploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W. C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16, 1–205 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1942">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Moretzsohn, M. C. et al. A study of the relationships of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its most closely related wild species using intron sequences and microsatellite markers. Ann. Bot. 111, 113–126 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1945">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Robledo, G., Lavia, G. I. &amp; Seijo, G. Species relations among wild Arachis species with the A genome as revealed by FISH mapping of rDNA loci and heterochromatin detection. Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 1295–1307 (2009)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1948">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Species relationships among the wild B genome of Arachis species (section Arachis) based on FISH mapping of rDNA loci and heterochromatin detection: a new proposal for genome arrangement. Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 1033–1046 (2010)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1948_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Moretzsohn, M. C. et al. A linkage map for the B-genome of Arachis (Fabaceae) and its synteny to the A-genome. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 40 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1951">32</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">24</a>). (It should be noted that, except for the assembled reference genotype of Tifrunner, these methods are not capable of detecting genome changes that result from balanced homeologous exchanges or chromosome rearrangements.)</p><p>Genetic exchange between ancestral genomes could be inferred towards the ends of colinear pairs of homeologous chromosomes. In these regions, the genome structure was not the expected AABB, but may be better described as AAAA or BBBB, that is, ‘tetrasomic’ conformations. The abrupt junctions of these segments indicate that they may have occurred by crossover (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">12</a> and 25; datasets 3–5 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1973">25</a></sup>). In Tifrunner, 14.8 Mb of the A genome has been transferred, in blocks, into B chromosomes, and 3.1 Mb of the B genome has been transferred, in blocks, into A chromosomes (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">7</a>). Most of these tetrasomic regions are at the very distal ends of chromosomes—for example, the lower regions of Arahy.02/Arahy.12, Arahy.04/Arahy.14, Arahy.06/Arahy.16 and the upper regions of Arahy.05/Arahy.15—and these were present in all of the <i>A. hypogaea</i> and <i>A. monticola</i> genotypes surveyed (but not in induced allotetraploids derived from the same diploid ancestral species; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig1">1</a>; dataset 4a,b in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e1993">25</a></sup>). However, in slightly more proximal regions, the tetrasomic regions were variable. Notably, in different accessions, in some genome regions, genetic exchange had occurred in opposite directions, creating AAAA structures in some accessions, and BBBB structures in others (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">25</a>; dataset 4a,b in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2004">25</a></sup>). Although clearly identifiable as A or B, these tetrasomic regions contain a significant number of SNPs that are characteristic of the corresponding subgenome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">7</a>). This may be the result of genetic exchange by gene conversion prior to the large-scale transfer of genetic material between subgenomes. The fine-scale patterns of these SNPs represent substantially fixed, or fossilized, genetic signals (‘fingerprints’) from past events. Their uniformity in all six botanical varieties and the wild counterpart of peanut <i>A. monticola</i> favors a single polyploid origin for the two species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig1">1f,g</a>; dataset 5 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2018">25</a></sup>). In Tifrunner, chromosome segments transferred between subgenomes mostly form tetrasomic regions, although one region at the lower end of Arahy.16 contains a chromosome segment with predominantly ancestral A genome characteristics that is absent from Arahy.06 itself (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">6</a>). This region on Arahy.16 forms a peculiar structure in which B and A homeologous chromosome segments are retained in tandem.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Visualizations of genome compositions of A. hypogaea, A. monticola and hybrids derived from the peanut’s ancestors."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Visualizations of genome compositions of <i>A. hypogaea</i>, <i>A. monticola</i> and hybrids derived from the peanut’s ancestors.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="442" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>–<b>e</b>, Overviews of genetic exchange between ancestral A and B genomes; <b>f</b>,<b>g</b><b>,</b> visualization of fine-scale exchange at the ends of chromosomes. In <b>a</b>–<b>e</b>, data are of log<sub>2</sub>-transformed values of ratios of mapping densities of whole-genome sequences onto 17,373 orthologous A/B gene pairs from <i>A. ipaensis</i> and <i>A. duranensis</i>, ordered according to chromosome number and position in <i>A. ipaensis</i>. Where values cluster around zero, as is the case in the diploid hybrid in <b>a</b>, A and B genes are present in equal number and are unaltered by genetic flux between them; in tetraploid genotypes this indicates a genome structure of AABB. Deviations from zero indicate genetic flux between the orthologous gene pairs, or complete replacement of A genes by B, or vice versa. <b>b</b>, The ninth generation tetraploid hybrid shows such deviations, with a change in genome structure from AABB to AAAA for chromosomes A04/B04 and the upper regions of B07/A08. <b>c</b>–<b>e</b>, These patterns are very different from those of <i>A. monticola</i> and <i>A. hypogaea</i>, which are similar to each other (and throughout diverse genotypes). Note deviations are mostly at chromosome ends. <b>f</b>,<b>g</b>, Fine-scale recombination (fingerprints) between A and B subgenomes are shown in two distal chromosome regions in which the genome structure approximates AAAA; the presence of SNPs characteristic of the ancestral B that form barcode-like patterns that are uniform in all <i>A. monticola</i> and <i>A. hypogaea</i> are observed. These patterns emphasize the similarities between <i>A. monticola</i> and <i>A. hypogaea</i> and favor a single polyploid origin (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">25</a>; dataset 4a in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2117">25</a></sup>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Structural variation generated by deletions and recombination between ancestral genomes (or subgenomes) of A. hypogaea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Structural variation generated by deletions and recombination between ancestral genomes (or subgenomes) of <i>A. hypogaea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="356" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>–<b>c</b>, Genetic exchange between Arahy.04 and Arahy.14 in three <i>A. hypogaea</i>, visualized by mapping the densities of short-read whole-genome sequences onto <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> (normalized values, in blue and red respectively; distances scaled to Arahy.14). Where mapping densities cluster around the expected value of one, the genome composition is AABB. Where mapping densities on one genome increase to approximately two and on the other genome decrease to near zero, the genome composition is better described as AAAA or BBBB. Mapping densities decrease to around zero on one genome and remain around one on the other indicate a deletion (common in <b>b</b>). <b>d</b>, A panel of 68 representative diverse genotypes in a region of Arahy.04 and Arahy.14 in which hypervariability has been created by differential recombination between subgenomes. The panel represents a heat map of log<sub>2</sub>-transformed ratios of mapping densities on the B and A genomes; blue represents AAAA and red represents BBBB, Tif., PI and Chib. are the genotypes repesented in <b>a</b>–<b>c</b>, respectively (for full visualizations, see dataset 4b in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2174">25</a></sup>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The signals of disperse genetic exchange were also detectable through the bodies of chromosomes. Overall, this dispersed genetic exchange has had a greater total effect than the transfer of chromosome segments. In Tifrunner, almost twice as many B alleles have been transferred to A chromosomes than vice versa (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">6</a>; dataset 3 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2192">25</a></sup>). In addition, variable deletions were frequent in proximal chromosome regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig2">2</a>; dataset 4c in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2199">25</a></sup>). Notably, a large deletion (around 10 Mb) was common on Arahy.14 of botanical varieties <i>fastigiata</i> and <i>vulgaris</i> (e.g., Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><p>In Tifrunner, genome deletions have disproportionately affected some gene families. The genes most frequently lost were members of the serine/threonine-protein phosphatase (around 89 genes) and FAR1-related families (around 83 genes). Genes in these families tend to occur in large genomic clusters or arrays, which can expand or contract through slipped-strand mispairing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Levinson, G. &amp; Gutman, G. A. Slipped-strand mispairing: a major mechanism for DNA sequence evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 4, 203–221 (1987)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2216">33</a></sup>. There have also been apparent increases in gene families; these include an increase of around 118 SAUR-like auxin-responsive protein family genes and around 50 NBS-LRR–encoding genes (the latter family of genes encode plant nucleotide-binding-site leucine-rich repeats and are associated with pest and disease resistance). For an overview of loss of ancestral SNP alleles through homeologous recombination and deletions in 39 diverse genotypes, see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">26</a>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec9">Mobile-element activity</h4><p>Transposable elements generate extrachromosomal circular DNAs when active<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lanciano, S. et al. Sequencing the extrachromosomal circular mobilome reveals retrotransposon activity in plants. PLoS Genet. 13, e1006630 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2231">34</a></sup>. Circular DNAs were detected from a MUTATOR (<i>MU4</i>) and <i>TY3-GYPSY</i> (<i>ZUHE</i>) element in <i>A. duranensis</i>, <i>A. ipaensis</i>, their hybrids and <i>A. hypogaea</i>, and from a <i>TY1-COPIA</i> element (<i>YARA</i>) in <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. hypogaea</i>. However, no abundant circular DNAs were detected in induced allotetraploids or <i>A. hypogaea</i> that were not detected in one or both of the ancestral diploids (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">27</a>). This indicates that after hybridization and polyploidy, somatic transposable element regulation was not heavily disturbed and no new large-scale mobilization of transposable elements occurred. Comparisons of genome sequences of the ancestral species and <i>A. hypogaea</i> support this; we could not identify any large-scale insertions. Consistent with previous findings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. Characterization of active miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements in the peanut genome. Theor. Appl. Genet. 124, 1429–1438 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2276">35</a></sup>, most newly inserted elements are MUTATOR-like elements (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">28</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec10">Inversions</h4><p>Comparisons of Tifrunner subgenomes showed three more major chromosome inversions than were observed when comparing the sequenced accessions of the two ancestral diploid species: two in the A subgenome, on Arahy.05 and Arahy.07, and one in the B subgenome, on Arahy.11 (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">8</a>). We consider it likely that at least two of these three extra inversions were already present in the diploid ancestors. The alternative chromosomal arrangement of Arahy.07 is indicated by a genetic map derived from a cross of two different <i>A. duranensis</i> accessions (see the genetic map of a previously published study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Nagy, E. D. et al. A high-density genetic map of Arachis duranensis, a diploid ancestor of cultivated peanut. BMC Genomics 13, 469 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2303">36</a></sup>, which is presented relative to the sequenced genome of <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 in the supplementary dataset of another study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2311">6</a></sup>). Furthermore, significantly higher DNA identity between Arahy.07 and five <i>A. duranensis</i> accessions (including the closest ones to the A subgenome ancestor; see below) is observed when compared to others (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">9</a>). Similarly, for Arahy.05, markedly higher identities to three <i>A. duranensis</i> accessions may indicate the presence of the inversion in some representatives of <i>A. duranensis</i>, possibly including the ancestral A subgenome donor (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>We previously reported that inversions move repeat-rich DNA to more distal chromosome regions where DNA is lost by recombination, thus reducing genome size (although regions moved to more proximal positions gain DNA, this effect is smaller)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2340">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ren, L., Huang, W., Cannon, E. K. S., Bertioli, D. J. &amp; Cannon, S. B. A mechanism for genome size reduction following genomic rearrangements. Front. Genet. 19, 454 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2343">37</a></sup>. Following this pattern, the inverted region in Arahy.05 has shrunk relative to <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 (tetraploid size/diploid size = 0.89; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">10</a>). We found that removal of LTR retrotransposons is the predominant cause of this reduction (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">29</a>). Furthermore, the presence of repeats in <i>A. duranensis</i>, at the ends of the regions, which are missing in <i>A. hypogaea</i>, clearly implicate unequal intrastrand recombination in about 20% of cases (107 out of 502 regions). By contrast, there is little difference in relative sizes of the inversions on Arahy.07 and Arahy.11.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Observations of independent polyploidy events</h3><p>We used allotetraploids derived by colchicine treatment of hybrids of the peanut’s ancestral diploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Fávero, A. P., Simpson, C. E., Valls, F. M. J. &amp; Velo, N. A. Study of evolution of cultivated peanut through crossability studies among Arachis ipaensis, A. duranensis and A. hypogaea. Crop Sci. 46, 1546–1552 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2372">38</a></sup> to investigate genome changes that followed independent polyploidy events. We studied 37 different lineages from two independent induced polyploidy events. Genetic exchange between subgenomes occurred in large blocks and interspersed alleles along chromosome segments; these events seem at least partly stochastic, and were different between different lineages and from <i>A. hypogaea</i>. Spontaneous changes in flower color in some lineages (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig3">3</a>) could be ascribed to genetic exchange between subgenomes; the A genome region that confers the yellow flower color had been replaced by the homeologous B genome region that confers orange flower color (dataset 6 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2382">25</a></sup>). This provides a simple demonstration of phenotypic change as a consequence of genetic exchange between subgenomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Homeologous recombination generates diversity in early generation tetraploid hybrids derived from peanut’s ancestors."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Homeologous recombination generates diversity in early generation tetraploid hybrids derived from peanut’s ancestors.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="457" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The initial allotetraploid, <i>A. ipaensis</i> × <i>A. duranensis</i> (2<i>n</i> = 4× = 40) has yellow flowers (left), as expected. However, after several generations some lineages spontaneously began to bear orange flowers (right). By genotyping, this could be assigned to homeologous recombination, where in alleles that confer yellow flowers (from <i>A. duranensis</i>) are replaced by alleles that confer orange flowers (from <i>A. ipaensis</i>; see dataset 6 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2414">25</a></sup>). Scale bar, 5 mm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">A closer representative of the A subgenome ancestor</h3><p>Because their seeds develop underground, wild <i>Arachis</i> populations are unusually static over time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W. C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16, 1–205 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2437">5</a></sup>. In addition, they typically have very high rates of self-pollination. This, and a serendipitous collection by pioneering botanical collectors, enabled our previous discovery that the sequenced <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 was very likely a descendant of the same population that donated the B subgenome to <i>A. hypogaea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2446">6</a></sup>. Here we endeavored to identify the extant <i>A. duranensis</i> population that is closest to the A subgenome donor. We characterized 55 accessions, representative of all known major populations of <i>A. duranensis</i>, by sequencing DNA enriched for genic regions (using exome capture methods). A selection of these accessions was chosen for whole-genome re-sequencing. The <i>A. duranensis</i> accessions that were most similar to the Tifrunner A subgenome were from Rio Seco (Argentina), a location previously indicated as the likely origin of the A subgenome ancestor on the basis of chloroplast and ribosomal DNA haplotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Grabiele, M., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Genetic and geographic origin of domesticated peanut as evidenced by 5S rDNA and chloroplast DNA sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 298, 1151–1165 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2460">39</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">11</a>). However, in some cases, the ranking of similarity changed by chromosome (especially for Arahy.05), possibly reflecting variations in chromosomal arrangements in different accessions of <i>A. duranensis</i> (as discussed above; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">9</a>).
