Comparisons of the Tifrunner A subgenome with the whole-genome sequences of <i>A. duranensis</i> accessions indicated median DNA identities of 99.76% for the Rio Seco accessions (KGBSPSc 30065, PI 468201 and KGBSPSc 30067, PI 468202); 99.61% for the sequenced V14167 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2486">6</a></sup>); and 98.23% for PI 475845 from the northern range of the species and with a partially assembled genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Chen, X. et al. Draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor (Arachis duranensis) provides insights into geocarpy, oil biosynthesis, and allergens. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 6785–6790 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2490">40</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">30</a><b>;</b> dataset 7 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2503">25</a></sup>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Similarity of A. duranensis from different locations to the A subgenome of Tifrunner."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Similarity of <i>A. duranensis</i> from different locations to the A subgenome of Tifrunner.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0405-z/MediaObjects/41588_2019_405_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="791" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Genomic DNAs of 55 accessions, representing all known major populations of <i>A. duranensis</i>, were compared to the A subgenome of Tifrunner. Similarity is strongly influenced by hydrographic basins. Accessions with the highest similarity (in red) are concentrated around Rio Seco, a tributary of the Rio San Francisco; next in similarity (in orange) are accessions concentrated around Jujuy, a region that drains into the Rio San Francisco and the Lerma valleys; followed by accessions from the Rio Juramento (in light brown), a region that receives water from the Lerma valley. Following these in similarity are accessions from the endorreic basins that occasionally drain in the Bermejo River (northwest Argentina and south Bolivia) (in yellow) followed by accessions in the basins of the Rio Pilcomayo (in light green) and finally accessions from the Rio Parapetí basin, Izozog Swamps and West Paraguay sand dunes (in dark green). Outliers to this general pattern are likely to represent populations that have resulted from the occasional human movement of seeds among basins (most of these movements are likely to have occurred long ago). The maps were generated using Natural Earth.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The A subgenome chromosomes are, in general, less similar to their <i>A. duranensis</i> counterparts than the B subgenome chromosomes are to their <i>A. ipaensis</i> counterparts. This is consistent with the greater flow of alleles from the B subgenome into the A subgenome than vice versa (as described above, see also a previously published study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2543">6</a></sup>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>A genome sequence is a landmark for the research of the biology of a crop. It provides a catalog of gene content, with chromosomal context and a unified framework for biological investigations and cross-species comparisons. In the case of peanut, a polyploid of recent hybrid origin, the previous sequencing of very close representatives of its diploid ancestors provides the opportunity to investigate more generally applicable principles regarding the genetics of polyploidy and its importance to crop domestication.</p><p>Polyploidy has long been recognized as an important feature of plant evolution; it has occurred multiple times during the evolution of almost all flowering plants. Following each polyploidy event, over tens of millions of years, deletions, divergence of duplicated genes and rearrangements return the genome to a diploid state. The recurrence of these ‘wondrous cycles’ is thought to have played an important part in diversification and adaptation during plant evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667–1678 (2004)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2559">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, D. E., Soltis, P. S. &amp; Tate, J. A. Advances in the study of polyploidy since plant speciation. New Phytol. 161, 173–191 (2004)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2559_1">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Wendel, J. F. The wondrous cycles of polyploidy in plants. Am. J. Bot. 102, 1753–1756 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2562">43</a></sup>. It has also long been recognized that many crop plants are recent polyploids; and, although the matter has generated decades of debate, it does seem that polyploids are favored for domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Hilu, K. W. Polyploidy and the evolution of domesticated plants. Am. J. Bot. 80, 1494–1499 (1993)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2566">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Salman-Minkov, A., Sabath, N. &amp; Mayrose, I. Whole-genome duplication as a key factor in crop domestication. Nat. Plants 2, 16115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2569">2</a></sup>.</p><p>We consider the evidence that polyploid <i>A. hypogaea</i> was favored for domestication over its diploid relatives very persuasive. Archaeological remains and remnant populations of <i>Arachis</i> species far from their natural distributions, and the existence of a diploid domesticated species (<i>A. villosulicarpa</i>) testify to widespread and large-scale cultivation of at least four diploid species (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a>). Indeed, the hybridization that gave rise to <i>A. hypogaea</i> was only possible because of human transport of <i>A. ipaensis</i> into the range of <i>A. duranensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2597">6</a></sup>. It seems important that, in spite of higher genetic diversity of the diploid species and their cultivation having started earlier, it was—in fact—the allotetraploid <i>A. hypogaea</i> that became the crop of worldwide importance.</p><p>Following trends seen in many plants, <i>Arachis</i> allotetraploids are larger than their diploid progenitors. The tetraploids also have different transpiration characteristics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Leal-Bertioli, S. C. et al. The effect of tetraploidization of wild Arachis on leaf morphology and other drought-related traits. Environ. and Exp. Bot 84, 17–24 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2610">44</a></sup> and produce more photosynthetic pigments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Leal‐Bertioli, S. C. et al. Phenotypic effects of allotetraploidization of wild Arachis and their implications for peanut domestication. Am. J. Bot. 104, 379–388 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2614">45</a></sup>. These traits—or other ploidy-related changes—may have been advantageous; however, contrary to common expectations, the seeds of the allotetraploid ancestor of peanut seem likely to have been similar size to those of its diploid progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Leal‐Bertioli, S. C. et al. Phenotypic effects of allotetraploidization of wild Arachis and their implications for peanut domestication. Am. J. Bot. 104, 379–388 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2618">45</a></sup>. The increased number of alleles associated with being a ‘fixed hybrid’ would have increased heterosis and therefore probably adaptability. However, the extreme genetic bottleneck that accompanied the polyploid origin may have been expected to reduce variability on which artificial selection could act. We investigated genome changes after polyploidy that could have generated variation. We found no evidence for widespread mobilization of transposable elements (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">27</a>). However, we could identify some mobile element insertion polymorphisms and some of these are likely to have influenced gene activity (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">28</a>). In addition, variable deletions, especially in proximal chromosome regions, have occurred since polyploidy and these also must have generated variation. However, it was a different genetic phenomenon, associated with harboring full chromosome complements from two species, that most drew our attention: genetic exchange between subgenomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Husted, L. Cytological studies of the peanut Arachis. II. Chromosome number, morphology and behavior, and their application to the problem of the origin of the cultivated forms. Cytologia 7, 396–423 (1936)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2629">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2632">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Leal-Bertioli, S. et al. Tetrasomic recombination is surprisingly frequent in allotetraploid Arachis. Genetics 199, 1093–1105 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2635">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Clevenger, J. et al. Genome-wide SNP genotyping resolves signatures of selection and tetrasomic recombination in peanut. Mol. Plant 10, 309–322 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2638">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2641">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, C. J. Homoeologous recombination in allopolyploids: the polyploid ratchet. New Phytol. 186, 18–28 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2644">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Hurgobin, B. et al. Homoeologous exchange is a major cause of gene presence/absence variation in the amphidiploid Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1265–1274 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2648">29</a></sup>.</p><p>We identified two patterns of homeologous recombination. One involves the transfer of chromosome segments between distal collinear regions of chromosomes mostly resulting in tetrasomic genome structures (AAAA and, to a lesser extent, BBBB; Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">25</a>). The other involves transfer of dispersed alleles that has occurred throughout the chromosomes; it is strongly biased, with much more transfer of alleles from B subgenome to A subgenome (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">7</a>). Overall, the genetic flux seems to have caused a greater erosion of similarity of the A subgenome to its progenitor <i>A. duranensis</i> than of the B subgenome to its progenitor <i>A. ipaensis</i> (even though the distal regions of the B chromosomes are more invaded by segments of the A genome than vice versa). Collections from Rio Seco were the closest representatives of the A subgenome ancestor, although several accessions from Salta (including the sequenced V14167 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2684">6</a></sup>)) showed quite similar degrees of identity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">11</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">30</a>).</p><p>On the whole-genome scale, the effects of homeologous recombination appear similar in diverse peanut accessions. Most of the tetrasomic structures were present in all <i>A. hypogaea</i> and <i>A. monticola</i> analyzed; furthermore, fingerprint-like fine-scale patterns of interspersed homeologous alleles within the distal tetrasomic regions were also found to be uniform (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig1">1</a>; datasets 4a and 5 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2716">25</a></sup>). By contrast, homeologous recombination patterns in allotetraploid hybrids were completely distinct (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig1">1</a>; dataset 4a,b in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2724">25</a></sup>). This emphasizes the close relationship of <i>A. hypogaea</i> and <i>A. monticola</i>, and favors a single polyploid origin of both species. However, when observed on a finer scale in other genome regions, it becomes apparent that homeologous recombination in <i>A. hypogaea</i> has generated new diversity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig2">2</a>). Some tetrasomic regions differ in different accessions of <i>A. hypogaea</i>; in certain genome regions some peanut accessions have an AAAA genome structure, whereas others have BBBB (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">25</a>). Our observation for flower color, although a simple trait, provides a proof-of-principle link between homeologous recombination and generation of phenotypic diversity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#Fig3">3</a>; dataset 6 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2753">25</a></sup>).</p><p>In summary, we determined the genome sequence of one reference peanut cultivar, and surveyed the genome structures of a diverse sample of landraces and cultivars. The genome structure of peanut is segmental allotetraploid (as defined by Stebbins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Stebbins, G. L. Types of polyploids: their classification and significance. Adv. Genet. 1, 403–429 (1947)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2760">46</a></sup>). We suggest that genetic deletions and exchange between the subgenomes generated variation that helped to favor the domestication of <i>A. hypogaea</i> over its diploid relatives. These results highlight a possible wider importance of these genetic mechanisms in accounting for the higher than expected frequency of polyploids in domesticated plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Plant material for genome sequencing</h3><p>To generate the reference genome, we used <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Holbrook, C. C. &amp; Culbreath, A. K. Registration of ‘Tifrunner’ peanut. J. Plant Regist. 1, 124 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2782">22</a></sup>, a runner-type peanut adapted for the southeast of the United States (CV-93, PI 644011). Phenotypically, Tifrunner would be classified as <i>A. hypogaea</i> ssp. <i>hypogaea</i> var. <i>hypogaea</i>, although, like almost all runner peanuts that are currently grown in the southeast United States, it has cultivars termed ‘Spanish’ in its pedigree (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">31</a>). Plants grown in an isolation plot in 2005 were genotyped with 146 simple sequence repeat DNA markers positioned on all 20 chromosomes. A line with no detected heterozygosity was used as the founder of the peanut genome project stock.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Sequencing of the reference tetraploid genome</h3><p>We sequenced <i>Arachis hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner using a whole-genome shotgun sequencing strategy and standard sequencing protocols. Illumina and PacBio reads were produced at USDA ARS GBRU and the HudsonAlpha Institute. Illumina reads were produced using the Illumina HiSeq platform and the PacBio reads were generated on the RSII platform. Two 800-bp insert 2× 250 Illumina fragment libraries were obtained for a total of 63.09× coverage. Before use, all Illumina reads were screened for mitochondria, chloroplast and PhiX contamination. Reads composed of &gt;95% simple sequences were removed. Illumina reads that were &lt;75 bp after trimming for adapter and quality (<i>Q</i> &lt; 20) were removed. An additional deduplication step was performed on the Illumina mate pairs that identifies and retains only one copy of each PCR duplicate. These two Illumina libraries were used in the final polishing of homozygous SNPs and insertions and deletions (indels) in the consensus sequence. For the PacBio sequencing, high-molecular weight DNA was isolated at the Arizona Genomics Institute (<a href="https://www.genome.arizona.edu">https://www.genome.arizona.edu</a>), a total of 301 chips (P6C4 chemistry) were sequenced with a total yield of 207.2 Gb (76.74×) and after error correction a total of 130.27 Gb (48.25×) was used in the assembly (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome assembly and construction of pseudomolecule chromosomes</h3><p>The 17,747,748 PacBio reads (76.74× sequence coverage) were assembled using MECAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Xiao, C. L. et al. MECAT: fast mapping, error correction, and de novo assembly for single-molecule sequencing reads. Nat. Methods 14, 1072–1074 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2834">47</a></sup>. This produced 7,692 contigs with an N50 of 696.6 kb, 4,778 larger than 100 kb and a total genome size of 2,502.6 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">14</a>). The resulting assembly was polished using Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2841">48</a></sup>. Three genetic maps (see below; dataset 8 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2845">25</a></sup>) were used to identify potential misjoined regions in the MECAT assembly. Synteny with <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> diploid references was then used to pinpoint breakpoints. A total of 856 potential misjoined regions were identified and broken.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Hi-C scaffolding</h3><p>The broken assembly was then scaffolded with Hi-C data using the 3D-DNA pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2864">20</a></sup>. We prepared two in situ Hi-C libraries as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Rao, S. S. et al. A 3D map of the human genome at kilobase resolution reveals principles of chromatin looping. Cell 159, 1665–1680 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2868">49</a></sup> and sequenced them (library 1: 62,762,161 of PE85 and 114,895,839 of PE150 reads; library 2: 228,896,977 of PE150; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">12</a>). The Hi-C reads were aligned to the broken assembly using the Juicer pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst. 3, 95–98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2875">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99–101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2878">51</a></sup>. The 3D-DNA pipeline was run with the following parameters: --editor-saturation-centile 10 --editor-coarse-resolution 100000 --editor-coarse-region 400000 --editor-repeat-coverage 50. The results were polished using the Juicebox Assembly Tools—an assembly-specific module in the Juicebox visualization system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. The Juicebox Assembly Tools module facilitates de novo assembly of mammalian genomes with chromosome-length scaffolds for under $1000. Preprint at bioRxiv &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1101/254797&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2882">52</a></sup>. The Hi-C scaffolding resulted in 20 chromosome-length scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Construction of pseudomolecule chromosomes</h3><p>An additional set of six breaks were made after scaffolding. Scaffolds were then oriented, ordered and joined together into 20 chromosomes. A total of 17 joins were made during this process, these chromosome joins were padded with 10,000 N characters. Telomeric sequences were identified using the (TTTAGGG)<sub><i>n</i></sub> repeat and care was taken to ensure correct orientation. Chromosomes were named according to subgenome, with the A subgenome being denoted as Arahy.01–Arahy.10 and the B subgenome denoted as Arahy.11–Arahy.20. Seven regions at the ends of chromosomes had autotetraploid-like genome structures and, as expected, were represented only once instead of twice. These were identified by twofold higher mapping densities on one subgenome of Tifrunner Illumina sequence reads (dataset 9 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2899">25</a></sup>). These seven regions, covering 16.6 Mb, were identified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">15</a>), duplicated and added into the other subgenome. A total of 99.30% of the assembled sequence is represented in the chromosomes.</p><p>The combined assembly was then screened for contamination. Homozygous SNPs and indels were corrected in the release sequence using ~60× of Illumina reads (2× 250, 800-bp insert size) by aligning the reads using BWA MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2909">53</a></sup> and identifying homozygous SNPs and indels with the GATK’s UnifiedGenotyper tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2913">54</a></sup>. A total of 90 homozygous SNPs and 134,361 homozygous indels were corrected in the release sequence. The final version contains 2,552.5 Mb of sequence, consisting of 384 scaffolds (4,037 contigs) with a contig N50 of 1.5 Mb and a total of 99.3% of assembled bases in chromosomes. A Hi-C contact map visualization of the completed assembly is shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">32</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Assessment of assembly accuracy</h3><p>A set of 223 bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) clones (20.8 Mb) were sequenced in order to assess the accuracy of the assembly. DNAs were extracted individually and then pooled into sets of 96 BACs. PACBIO RS II was used for sequencing with a targeted 100× depth. Assembly of the pools was performed using HGAP3 (version 2.3.0) followed by consensus sequence calling with Quiver (version 2.1). Vectors were identified and trimmed, clones recircularized and repolished with Quiver to obtain the final BAC contigs. A range of variants were detected in the comparison of the BAC clone contigs and the genome assembly. Two of the BAC contigs were excluded, because they aligned to highly repetitive pericentromeric regions, and 46 of the contigs were excluded based on length (≤20 kb), leaving 175 contig alignments for analysis. Of these, a total of 79 alignments were of high quality (&lt;0.1% bp error; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">33</a>); dot plots were generated using Gepard<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Krumsiek, J., Arnold, R. &amp; Rattei, T. Gepard: a rapid and sensitive tool for creating dotplots on genome scale. Bioinformatics 23, 1026–1028 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2931">55</a></sup>. The next 86 BAC contigs indicate a higher error rate, which was mainly due to their placement in more repetitive regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">34</a>). The final ten BAC contigs indicate putative overlaps on adjacent contigs within a chromosome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">35</a>). The overall bp error rate (including marked gap bases) in the BAC clone contigs is 1 error per 33,510 bp (431 discrepant bp out of 14,442,956).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Mapping populations, genotyping and linkage maps</h3><p>The <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner × <i>A. hypogaea</i> GT-C20 population was composed of 91 <i>F</i><sub>8</sub> individuals derived by single-seed descent and was used for mapping, whole-genome sequencing and marker calling as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Agarwal, G. et al. High-density genetic map using whole-genome resequencing for fine mapping and candidate gene discovery for disease resistance in peanut. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1954–1967 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2959">56</a></sup>. Joinmap 5.0 was used for genetic map construction after selecting markers without segregation distortion (<i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> test; <i>P</i> &gt; 0.05; 1:1 ratio of alleles), using the Kosambi mapping function and a minimum logarithm of odds score for linkage of 10.</p><p>The <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Runner IAC 886 × (<i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 × <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167)<sup>(2<i>n</i> = 4× = 40)</sup> population consists of 89 <i>F</i><sub>6</sub> individuals that were derived by single-seed descent. The linkage map has been described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e2994">6</a></sup>.</p><p>The <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Runner IAC 886 × (<i>A. batizocoi</i> K9484 × <i>A. stenosperma</i> V10309)<sup>(2<i>n</i> = 4× = 40)</sup> consists of a population of 196 <i>F</i><sub>2</sub> individuals. Genotyping for SNPs was done using the Affymetrix genotyping array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Clevenger, J. et al. Genome-wide SNP genotyping resolves signatures of selection and tetrasomic recombination in peanut. Mol. Plant 10, 309–322 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3021">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Pandey, M. K. et al. Development and evaluation of a high density genotyping ‘Axiom_Arachis’ array with 58 K SNPs for accelerating genetics and breeding in groundnut. Sci. Rep. 7, 40577 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3024">57</a></sup>. Maps were constructed using the Kosambi function in Mapdisto<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Lorieux, M. MapDisto: fast and efficient computation of genetic linkage maps. Mol. Breed. 30, 1231–1235 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3028">58</a></sup> version 2.0; 20% of missing data was allowed, with a minimum logarithm of odds score of 20 and a maximum recombination frequency of 0.30.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Identification of repetitive DNA</h3><p>Mobile elements were identified using a number of homology and de novo structural pattern-finding algorithms and manual curation; see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Structural comparisons of chromosomes</h3><p>Structural comparisons between chromosomes were generated and visualized using the MUMmer suite of alignment tools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3051">59</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Assembly of transcripts and gene annotation</h3><p>A transcriptome assembly to support annotation was generated from more than 6.4 billion cleaned sequence reads from <i>A. hypogaea</i> ssp. <i>hypogaea</i> genotypes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">16</a>). Libraries were constructed and 100- or 125-bp paired-end sequences generated following recommendations of the manufacturer (Illumina). Assembly was carried out with Trinity using the tetraploid genome as a guide. Read redundancy was first reduced with Trinity in silico normalization, with --max_cov 100, giving 97 million normalized reads. The normalized reads were aligned to the Tifrunner genome assembly using gsnap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Nacu, S. Fast and SNP-tolerant detection of complex variants and splicing in short reads. Bioinformatics 26, 873–881 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3072">60</a></sup> and then assembled using Trinityrnaseq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494–1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3076">61</a></sup> version 2.5.0, with maximum intron size of 10,000, and <i>k</i>-mer minimum coverage of 3. After filtering transcript assemblies using Kallisto<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Bray, N. L., Pimentel, H., Melsted, P. &amp; Pachter, L. Near-optimal probabilistic RNA-seq quantification. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 525–527 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3084">62</a></sup> (transcripts per million of 1.5; 90,519 assembled transcripts were retained, with an average size of 1,540 nucleotides.</p><p>The <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner genome was annotated using the MAKER pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3094">63</a></sup> version 2.31.9 (specifically, the dockerized image maker-2.31.9-3.img run under singularity 2.4). The genome sequence was hard-masked for ‘complex repeats’ (for example, transposable elements) using RepeatMasker and a library of repeat sequences identified in <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3103">6</a></sup> and <i>A. hypogaea</i> (this manuscript). Simple repeats were soft-masked by MAKER, allowing them to be accessible for gene annotation in some cases. Ab initio gene prediction methods used within MAKER included SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3111">64</a></sup> version 2006-07-28 and AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3115">65</a></sup> version 3.2.3. <i>Arachis</i>-specific model parameters for the ab initio predictors were obtained initially from gene model calls made against chromosomes Arahy.01 and Arahy.11 (representing contributions from the two diploid progenitor species) by using only the highest-confidence gene models produced in a first iteration of the pipeline (annotated edit distance ≤ 0.25); this subset was used to train the predictors for the model parameters used in subsequent iterations of the full annotation process (four iterations in total). Protein sequences used as queries for homology-based predictions consisted of the Uniprot Fabaceae protein set (retrieved December 2017). Nucleotide sequences used as queries for homology-based predictions consisted of two transcriptome assemblies generated from <i>A. hypogaea</i> Tifrunner: the genome-guided transcriptome assembly described above, and the 22-tissue transcriptome assembly that has been described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Clevenger, J., Chu, Y., Scheffler, B. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. A developmental transcriptome map for allotetraploid Arachis hypogaea. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 1446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3125">66</a></sup>. Provisional functional assignments for the gene models were produced using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3129">67</a></sup> and BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421–430 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3134">68</a></sup> against annotated proteins from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Glycine max</i> and <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, with outputs processed using AHRD (<a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>), for lexical analysis and selection of the best functional descriptor of each gene product.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Comparison of gene expression in subgenomes</h3><p>Paired-end sequencing data from expressed RNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Clevenger, J., Chu, Y., Scheffler, B. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. A developmental transcriptome map for allotetraploid Arachis hypogaea. Front. Plant Sci. 7, 1446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3163">66</a></sup> was quality trimmed (<i>Q</i> ≥ 25) and reads shorter than 50 bp after trimming were discarded. Sequences were then aligned to the <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner genome and counts of reads uniquely mapping to annotated genes were obtained using STAR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15–21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3173">69</a></sup> version 2.5.3a. Outliers among the individual experimental samples were verified based on the Pearson correlation coefficient, <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> ≥ 0.85. Fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped values were calculated for each gene by normalizing the read count data to both the length of the gene and the total number of mapped reads in the sample and considered as the metric for estimating gene expression levels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3182">70</a></sup>. Normalized count data was obtained using the relative log expression (RLE) method in DESeq2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3186">71</a></sup>) version 1.14.1. Genes with low expression were filtered out, by requiring ≥2 RLE-normalized counts in at least two samples for each gene.</p><p>High-confidence homeologous gene pairs were initially identified by their reciprocal highest scores in similarity searches (BLAT) of all annotated genes in each Tifrunner subgenome versus the other. We also applied the criteria of a minimum of 80% nucleotide identity and 80% sequence length coverage and only considered gene pairs that reside on homeologous chromosomes and established reciprocal translocations (dataset 1a in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3193">25</a></sup>). We performed differential expression analysis between the genes in homeologous pairs for each tissue and pod developmental stage using DESeq2 (version 1.14.1) with log<sub>2</sub>-transformed expression ratio ≥1 and Benjamini–Hochberg-adjusted <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05 as the statistical cut-off for asymmetrically expressed genes. We used Gene Ontology for functional analysis of asymmetrically expressed homeolgous gene pairs. To determine overrepresented Gene Ontology categories across biological processes, cellular component and molecular function domains, topGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Alexa, A., Rahnenführer, J. &amp; Lengauer, T. Improved scoring of functional groups from gene expression data by decorrelating GO graph structure. Bioinformatics 22, 1600–1607 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3202">72</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Alexa, A. &amp; Rahnenfuhrer, J. topGO: enrichment analysis for gene ontology. R version 2 2240 (2016); &#xA;                    https://www.r-project.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3205">73</a></sup>, an R Bioconductor package was used. Enrichment of Gene Ontology terms was tested using Fisher’s exact test with <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05 considered as significant. Statistical analyses and visualizations were performed using the R version 3.4.1 statistical software (R Development Core Team 2011).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">DNA methylation</h3><p>Genomic DNA was isolated from whole young unexpanded leaves using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen). MethylC-sequencing libraries were constructed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Urich, M. A., Nery, J. R., Lister, R., Schmitz, R. J. &amp; Ecker, J. R. MethylC-seq library preparation for base-resolution whole-genome bisulfite sequencing. Nat. Protoc. 10, 475–483 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3220">74</a></sup>. In brief, approximately 1 μg of genomic DNA spiked with about 10 ng of unmethylated lambda DNA was sonicated to around 200 bp using a Covaris S-2. Size selection was performed using magnetic purification beads. The End-It DNA End-Repair Kit (Epicentre) was used to perform end repair on the fragmented DNA. A-tails were added to blunt-end fragments using Klenow 3′–5′ exonuclease and dA-Tailing Buffer (New England Biolabs). Methylated NEXTflex DNA adapters (Bio Scientific) were then ligated onto the DNA using T4 DNA ligase (New England Biolabs). Bisulfite conversion was done using the MethylCode Bisulfite Conversion Kit (Invitrogen). Finally, eight rounds of PCR using Kapa HiFi Uracil and Hotstart DNA polymerase (Kapa Biosystems) was used to amplify the libraries. Between each reaction, magnetic purification beads were used to clean up the DNA. Libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500.</p><p>Quality-trimmed reads were aligned to the <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner genome using Bismark<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Krueger, F. &amp; Andrews, S. R. Bismark: a flexible aligner and methylation caller for Bisulfite-Seq applications. Bioinformatics 27, 1571–1572 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3230">75</a></sup> version 0.7.0. Multiple mapped reads and clonal reads that corresponded to potential bias from PCR amplification were discarded. The first and last 5 bp of each read where masked before methylation calling to remove biases in methylation levels introduced during the end-repairing step of library preparation. Cytosine methylation levels were calculated using the binomial distribution as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Lister, R. et al. Human DNA methylomes at base resolution show widespread epigenomic differences. Nature 462, 315–322 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3234">76</a></sup>. The bisulfite non-conversion rate was calculated by mapping the unmapped reads to the unmethylated lambda genome. Only cytosines covered by at least three reads in at least one of the two replicates were retained and the two replicates were then merged for further analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Small RNAs</h3><p>Low-molecular-weight RNAs were separated from total cellular RNAs extracted using Direct-zol RNA MiniPrep (Zymo Research) as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Lu, C., Meyers, B. C. &amp; Green, P. J. Construction of small RNA cDNA libraries for deep sequencing. Methods 43, 110–117 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3246">77</a></sup>. Libraries were prepared with TruSeq Small RNA Library Preparation Kit (Illumina) and sequenced using NextSeq (Illumina).</p><p>Small RNA reads from three replicates were trimmed for adapters and quality using cutadapt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Martin, M. Cutadapt removes adapter sequences from high-throughput sequencing reads. EMBnet J. 17, 10 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3253">78</a></sup> and merged into a single non-redundant small RNA library. Small RNA reads of 21-, 22- and 24-nucleotide length were then mapped to the <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner genome using Bowtie2. For each read, all alignments where reported using the -a option in Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3260">79</a></sup>. Only perfectly matched reads were kept for further analysis. Unique small RNA reads were defined as reads that perfectly aligned to a single location in the reference genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Diverse genotypes for analysis of genome structures</h3><p>A diverse panel of more than 200 tetraploid genotypes that represent the wild <i>A. monticola</i>, all six botanical varieties of the cultivated <i>A. hypogaea</i>, modern varieties and induced allotetraploid hybrids of peanut’s ancestral species <i>A. duranensis</i> (V14167) and <i>A. ipaensis</i> (K30076) were sequenced using Illumina short (100–250 bp) paired-end sequencing (dataset 2 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3284">25</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Investigating genetic exchange between subgenomes</h3><p>Genetic exchange between the subgenomes was inferred by different methods: observing mapping densities of Illumina whole-genome sequences onto the combined sequenced diploid ancestral species genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3296">6</a></sup>; and by analysis of SNPs that consistently differentiate representatives of A and B genome diploid species.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec30">Mapping densities onto the combined diploid ancestral species genomes</h4><p>The methodology that uses mapping densities takes advantage of the diploid genome sequences being very similar to the corresponding subgenomes of <i>A. hypogaea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3309">6</a></sup>. After quality filtering, sequences were assigned to A or B genomes by mapping to the combined chromosomal pseudomolecule sequences of <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 and <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3319">6</a></sup>) using Bowtie2 version 2.2.9 with the -sensitive -local option. Mpileup files were generated using SamTools version 1.3, which were parsed to create average mapping densities for defined windows dividing the chromosomes. Three types of windows were used. First were windows defined by the annotated start and stop positions of 17,373 high-confidence orthologous gene pairs from the two diploids (dataset 4a in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3323">25</a></sup>). Second were pairs of windows defined using syntenous regions of the diploid genomes identified using DAGchainer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Haas, B. J., Delcher, A. L., Wortman, J. R. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3328">80</a></sup>; taking into account the relative orientation of the blocks and any difference in size, syntenous blocks were divided into corresponding A–B windows of approximately 10 kb (dataset 4b in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3332">25</a></sup>). Third were fixed-size 10-kb windows (dataset 4c in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3336">25</a></sup>). Normalized mapping densities (and for the first and second windows, log<sub>2</sub><sub>-</sub>normalized values of ratios of mapping densities) when displayed graphically across chromosomal sequences allow the genome compositions of tetraploid genotypes to be visualized.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec31">SNPs that consistently differentiate A and B genomes</h4><p>We chose <i>Arachis</i> species and accessions within the A and B genome groups with well-defined relationships: <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076, <i>A. magna</i> K30097 Krapov., W.C. Gregory &amp; C.E. Simpson, <i>A. valida</i> V9157 Krapov. &amp; W.C. Gregory, <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167, <i>A. duranensis</i> K36003, <i>A. duranensis</i> K30077 and <i>A. cardenasii</i> GKP10017 Krapov. &amp; W.C. Gregory. For an overview of their relationships, see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">24</a>.</p><p>Whole-genome Illumina sequences from these diploid species/accessions were mapped separately onto the Tifrunner A subgenome chromosomes and the B subgenome chromosomes. Variants were called using SamTools mpileup. SNPs that are diagnostic of the A and B genomes were identified as sites that differentiated all detected A species from the B species. For analysis of the diverse tetraploid genotypes, Illumina whole-genome sequences from each tetraploid accession were mapped to the A subgenome chromosomes and the B subgenome chromosomes separately. The alignment files were used to count the alleles at each diagnostic site using the pysam module in biopython.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec32">Homeologous exchange in polyploid hybrids made from peanut’s ancestral species</h4><p>We investigated homeologous exchange in induced allotetraploid <i>A. duranensis</i> × <i>A. ipanesis</i> (2<i>n</i> = 4× = 40) individuals by genotyping using the Affymetrix Axiom_Arachis Array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Clevenger, J. et al. Genome-wide SNP genotyping resolves signatures of selection and tetrasomic recombination in peanut. Mol. Plant 10, 309–322 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3399">16</a></sup>. By reference to the diploid parental controls, alleles could be assigned to the A and B genomes, enabling identification of genotype calls that represented AABB, AAAA and BBBB genome structures (dataset 6 in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bertioli, D. Supplementary material for &#34;The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea&#34;. CyVerse Data Commons &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3403">25</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Circular DNA and active transposable elements</h3><p>For descriptions of the methods used for identifying active transposable elements, see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec34">Identification of the A subgenome ancestor</h3><p>For this analysis, we used representatives from every known major population of <i>A. duranensis</i>. To compile these, we drew on knowledge of the distributions and characteristics of populations that has been built up during botanical expeditions and research over more than 50 years (much of this documented in a previous study by Krapovickas and Gregory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W. C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16, 1–205 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3431">5</a></sup>). Representatives of most populations were available from the USDA National Genetic Resources Program (USDA Germplasm Resources Information Network; <a href="https://www.ars-grin.gov">https://www.ars-grin.gov</a>), these were supplemented with DNA samples from accessions held at the Instituto de Botánica del Nordeste, Embrapa Recursos Genéticos e Biotecnologia and the International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics. In total, DNA from 55 accessions, plus some control species were used (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><p>An exome-capture bait set (SeqCap EZ Developer; Nimblegen/Roche) was designed for 30,460 genes, including untranslated regions, annotated in the <i>A. ipaensis</i> genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut. Nat. Genet. 48, 438–446 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17018e3451">6</a></sup> and 6,993 <i>A. hypogaea</i> SNPs, the majority of which had been identified previously by genotyping by sequencing. The bait set represents a capture region of about 50.14 Mb in diploid peanuts.</p><p>Barcode-indexed sequencing libraries were generated from genomic DNA samples sheared on an E220 Focused Ultrasonicator (Covaris), For each sample, 1 μg of sheared DNA was converted to sequencing libraries using a Kapa Hyper Library Preparation Kit (Kapa Biosystems/Roche). The exome-capture analysis was carried out with SeqCap EZ capture reagents according to the recommendations of the manufacturer (Nimblegen/Roche). Subsequently, 18 libraries were pooled before exome capture and sequenced on one Illumina HiSeq 4000 (Illumina) lane with paired-end 150-bp reads.</p><p>Sequencing data were mapped to the Tifrunner A subgenome chromosomes using BWA MEM with default parameters. Variants were called using SamTools mpileup and bcftools call (bcftools call -vc). Only variants that were called ‘homozygous alternative’ were considered. To normalize for missing data among lines across observed variant sites, similarity to the Tifrunner A subgenome was calculated as observed sites at which there is read coverage and a genotype score as homozygous reference divided by the total number of observed sites with read coverage. This strategy controls for differences in covered sites among the accessions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec35">Statistical analysis</h3><p>For a description of the statistical analyses, see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0405-z#MOESM1">3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec36">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the Nature Research Reporting Summary linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The datasets generated during and/or analyzed for this study are available in the public repository of the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI; <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov</a>) and/or the open access internet sites PeanutBase (<a href="https://peanutbase.org/">https://peanutbase.org/</a>). Genome assemblies and annotations, identified transposable elements, transcript assemblies, base methylation states and map data are available at Peanutbase (<a href="https://peanutbase.org/peanut_genome">https://peanutbase.org/peanut_genome</a>). <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner sequence reads are archived in the NCBI under BioProject accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA419393">PRJNA419393</a>, the genome assembly has GenBank accession numbers <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/gquery/?term=CM009801">CM009801</a>–<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/gquery/?term=CM009820">CM009820</a>. Small RNA sequences are deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under accession numbers <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SAMN06658954">SAMN06658954</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SAMN06658955">SAMN06658955</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SAMN06658956">SAMN06658956</a>. Datasets 1–9, as cited in manuscript, are deposited at <a href="https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74">https://doi.org/10.25739/hb5x-wx74</a>, Cyverse (<a href="http://datacommons.cyverse.org/browse/iplant/home/shared/commons_repo/curated/Bertioli_Arachis_genome_supplement_TVDM_Mar2019">http://datacommons.cyverse.org/browse/iplant/home/shared/commons_repo/curated/Bertioli_Arachis_genome_supplement_TVDM_Mar2019</a>) and PeanutBase (<a href="https://peanutbase.org/data/public/Arachis_hypogaea/Tifrunner.esm.TVDM/">https://peanutbase.org/data/public/Arachis_hypogaea/Tifrunner.esm.TVDM/</a>). Whole-genome sequencing data of diverse accessions are deposited in the NCBI under BioProject accession numbers <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA525866">PRJNA525866</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA511155">PRJNA511155</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA490832">PRJNA490832</a>.</p>

Clevenger and B.A. Data preparation and data basing, visualizations and expression analysis: S.B.C., E.K.S.C. and W.H. Genotyping and linkage mapping: C.B.-T., C. Chavarro, Y.C., J. Diverse tetraploid samples and data: S.C.M.L.-B., X.Z., Z.Z., Z.S., A.C., M.K.P., R.K.V., K.S., P.O.-A., S.A.J. D.J.B. and C. Chavarro. Analysis of variations in tetraploid genome structure: B.A., J. Clevenger, W.K. and D.J.B. Curation and sequencing of <i>A. duranensis</i> accessions: S.S.S., G.S., S.C.M.L.-B. and D.J.B. Exome capture and analysis: L.F., R.M., S.S., S.S.S., G.S., J. Clevenger, S.C.M.L.-B. and D.J.B. Biogeography: G.S., S.S.S. and D.J.B. Manuscript: D.J.B., G.S., S.B.C, M.M., J.J., P.O.-A., J. Campbell, J. Clevenger, S.C.M.L.-B, R.M., D.G., M.E., S.S.S., L.R. and S.A.J.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
